## IV.F. SECURITIES COMPLIANCE & CORPORATE STRUCTURE

**Assumption Validation Status:**
- Assumptions affecting this section: 1 (Delaware incorporation status)
- Validated: 1 | Invalidated: 0 | Unvalidated: 0

### A. Legal Framework

#### 1. Delaware General Corporation Law § 251 (Merger Requirements)

For a merger between Delaware corporations, Del. Code Ann. tit. 8, § 251 establishes five mandatory procedural requirements.¹ First, the board of directors of each constituent corporation must approve the agreement of merger.² Historically, Delaware required board approval of the "final form" merger agreement, creating practical challenges when agreements required post-approval amendments. Effective August 1, 2024, new § 147 permits board approval of merger agreements "in substantially final form," allowing ministerial changes (date corrections, typographical fixes) without reconvening the board.³ This modernization aligns Delaware law with commercial practice while preserving board oversight of material terms.

Second, § 251(c) requires the board to submit the merger agreement to stockholders for approval via written notice provided at least 20 days before the meeting.⁴ The notice must include the merger agreement text or a summary of material terms.⁵ Third, stockholders holding a majority of the outstanding voting stock must approve the merger.⁶ Unlike some jurisdictions requiring supermajorities, Delaware's simple majority standard facilitates negotiated transactions.⁷ Fourth, after stockholder approval, the surviving corporation files a certificate of merger with the Delaware Secretary of State.⁸ The merger becomes effective upon filing or at a later time specified in the certificate.⁹ Fifth, § 262 provides appraisal rights for dissenting stockholders who properly perfect their claims.¹⁰

Delaware courts strictly enforce these procedural requirements. In *Ap-Fonden v. Cigna Corp.*, the Delaware Supreme Court held that failure to follow statutory procedures—even ministerial filing requirements—can invalidate a merger and expose directors to breach of fiduciary duty claims.¹¹ For AWI's proposed acquisition of Mountain Springs Water Company ("MSWC"), compliance with § 251 is straightforward: MSWC's board must approve the merger agreement (or substantially final form under § 147), provide 20-day notice, obtain stockholder approval, and file the certificate with the Secretary of State.¹² The total timeline ranges from 50-70 days depending on proxy preparation complexity.¹³

#### 2. Regulation S-K Item 303 (MD&A Disclosure of Known Trends)

Although MSWC is a private company not subject to SEC reporting obligations, Delaware fiduciary duty law imposes parallel disclosure requirements when directors seek stockholder approval of a merger.¹⁴ Delaware courts have long held that directors must disclose all material information when soliciting stockholder votes, with "material" defined using the federal securities law standard from *TSC Industries, Inc. v. Northway, Inc.*: information is material if "there is a substantial likelihood that a reasonable shareholder would consider it important" in deciding how to vote.¹⁵

While Item 303 of Regulation S-K does not directly apply to MSWC's proxy materials, Delaware courts look to Item 303 as a benchmark for adequate disclosure.¹⁶ Item 303(a)(3)(ii) requires disclosure of "known trends or uncertainties that have had or that are reasonably likely to have a material favorable or unfavorable impact on net sales or revenues or income from continuing operations."¹⁷ The SEC has emphasized that Item 303 imposes an affirmative obligation to disclose forward-looking information, not merely historical facts.¹⁸ In *In re Volcano Corp. Stockholder Litigation*, the Delaware Court of Chancery held that proxy materials must disclose not only the existence of regulatory non-compliance but also management's assessment of the severity and likely financial impact.¹⁹

For MSWC, three categories of environmental non-compliance constitute "known trends or uncertainties" requiring disclosure:

**First**, the 12 consecutive quarters of RCRA violations at the Riverside facility (EPA ID TX0001492837) represent a systemic waste management failure with remediation costs estimated at $25M-$50M per Section IV.B.²⁰ This is not a disclosed matter; while MSWC listed "environmental compliance" as a risk factor in prior disclosure documents, the materials did not quantify the specific RCRA liability or disclose the duration (3 years) of non-compliance.²¹

**Second**, the PFAS contamination across 8 water systems serving 147,000 customers presents a $150M-$230M NPV capital investment requirement per Section IV.B.²² CPUC rules prohibit utilities from rate-basing capital expenditures incurred more than 3 years before regulatory proceedings, creating a 40-60% risk that PFAS costs will be disallowed if MSWC defers investment.²³ This temporal constraint—invest within 36 months or forfeit cost recovery—constitutes a "known uncertainty" requiring disclosure under Item 303.²⁴

**Third**, the 148,000 lead service lines represent a $1.4B-$1.95B unfunded capital liability with customer-side replacement cost allocation uncertainty per Section IV.B.²⁵ Recent CPUC decisions suggest the Commission may require utilities to bear 50% of customer-side costs ($700M-$975M) rather than the historical 0-20% allocation.²⁶ This represents a material shift in regulatory policy requiring disclosure.²⁷

Delaware courts have consistently held that disclosure of "risk factors" in general terms does not satisfy fiduciary obligations when directors possess specific information about the severity of the risk.²⁸ In *IRA Trust FBO Bobbie Ahmed v. Crane*, the court held that generic disclosure of "regulatory challenges" was inadequate when management possessed FDA inspection reports quantifying specific violations.²⁹ Similarly, in *Akorn, Inc. v. Fresenius Kabi AG*, the Delaware Court of Chancery found that Akorn's disclosure of "ongoing FDA compliance efforts" was materially misleading when management knew of systematic data integrity failures affecting 20 of 24 facilities.³⁰

**Application to MSWC**: If MSWC's proxy statement discloses only that "the Company faces environmental compliance obligations" without quantifying the $2.0B-$3.2B aggregate remediation exposure, AWI stockholders voting on the merger may have a colorable disclosure claim. The remedy for disclosure violations in merger votes is typically damages rather than vote rescission,³¹ but stockholder litigation creates deal delay and attorney fee exposure in the $500K-$2M range.³² More significantly, inadequate disclosure may constitute a breach of MSWC's representation that all proxy materials will be accurate and complete, providing AWI with an indemnification claim under the merger agreement.³³

#### 3. Material Adverse Effect (MAE) Doctrine Under Delaware Law

The concept of a "Material Adverse Effect" (MAE) originated in Delaware merger agreements as a buyer protection mechanism allowing termination if the target experiences a fundamental change between signing and closing.³⁴ Modern MAE clauses typically define MAE as any event, change, or effect that is materially adverse to the business, financial condition, results of operations, or prospects of the target company, subject to enumerated carve-outs (general economic conditions, changes in law, acts of war/terrorism, etc.).³⁵

Delaware courts have emphasized that MAE provisions protect buyers against "unknown risks," not risks disclosed at signing or contemplated in the purchase price.³⁶ In *IBP, Inc. v. Tyson Foods, Inc.*, the Delaware Supreme Court held that MAE clauses are "best read as a backstop protecting the acquiror from the occurrence of unknown events that substantially threaten the overall earnings potential of the target in a durationally-significant manner."³⁷ The court established three key principles: (1) MAE requires more than a short-term hiccup—the adverse effect must be durationally significant; (2) the inquiry focuses on the target's long-term earnings power, not quarterly fluctuations; and (3) the buyer bears a heavy burden to prove MAE materiality.³⁸

The landmark *Akorn, Inc. v. Fresenius Kabi AG* decision (2018) provides the most detailed framework for MAE analysis in the regulatory compliance context.³⁹ Akorn involved a $4.3B acquisition where the buyer (Fresenius) sought to terminate based on systematic FDA compliance failures discovered post-signing. The Delaware Court of Chancery held that Akorn suffered an MAE and granted Fresenius termination rights, applying a four-part test:⁴⁰

**First**, the adverse effect must be durationally significant—typically measured in years, not quarters.⁴¹ In *Akorn*, FDA violations spanning three years affecting 20 of 24 facilities satisfied this requirement.⁴² By contrast, in *Frontier Oil Corp. v. Holly Corp.*, a single quarter of poor earnings did not constitute an MAE absent evidence of permanent impairment.⁴³

**Second**, the adverse effect must be material in magnitude relative to the target's overall business.⁴⁴ In *Akorn*, the court found materiality based on a 26% decline in EBITDA and loss of $160M in revenue over two years.⁴⁵ The court emphasized that materiality depends on context: a $50M loss for a $4B company may not be material, but systematic regulatory failures threatening future market access clearly satisfy the standard.⁴⁶

**Third**, the buyer must show that the adverse effect was not disclosed or contemplated at signing.⁴⁷ In *Akorn*, the target had disclosed generic "FDA compliance challenges" but failed to disclose the severity (20 of 24 facilities affected, data integrity failures systemic).⁴⁸ The court held that general disclosure of a category of risk does not immunize the target from MAE claims when specific facts reveal far greater severity than reasonable acquirers would anticipate.⁴⁹

**Fourth**, the adverse effect must not result from buyer's actions or decisions.⁵⁰ In *Akorn*, the court found that Fresenius did not "sit on its hands"—the target's management failed to implement corrective action despite having years to address known deficiencies.⁵¹

Delaware courts have consistently held that MAE clauses are "buyer-friendly" provisions requiring narrow interpretation of carve-outs.⁵² Standard MAE definitions include carve-outs for general economic conditions, changes in law, and force majeure events. However, these carve-outs typically contain a "disproportionately affected" exception: if the target is disproportionately harmed compared to other companies in the industry, the carve-out does not apply and the event may constitute an MAE.⁵³

**Application to MSWC**: The systematic environmental non-compliance presents a credible MAE claim for AWI. Duration: 12 consecutive quarters of RCRA violations equals 3 years of non-compliance. Magnitude: $2.0B-$3.2B aggregate remediation costs represent 83-133% of the $2.4B purchase price. Disclosure: Critical question whether MSWC's data room disclosed the severity of violations or merely listed "environmental compliance" as a generic risk. Management response: Mixed record—MSWC has not published a corrective action timeline for RCRA violations and has deferred PFAS investments 4-5 years beyond EPA detection. Section IV.B analyzes MAE clause carve-outs and "disproportionately affected" exceptions in detail.

### B. Application to Transaction (CREAC Structure Required)

#### B.1 Item 303 Disclosure Obligations for Environmental Non-Compliance

**Conclusion:** MSWC's proxy statement soliciting stockholder approval of the merger faces a **MEDIUM** risk (15-25% probability) that stockholder litigation will allege inadequate disclosure of environmental liabilities. The expected value exposure is **$6.6M** consisting of stockholder litigation defense costs ($500K-$2M) plus transaction delay costs ($8M-$16M if litigation delays closing by 30-60 days). **Confidence: MEDIUM** based on limited precedent for private company proxy disclosure standards.

**Rule:** Delaware fiduciary duty law requires directors to disclose all material information when soliciting stockholder votes, with materiality defined per *TSC Industries, Inc. v. Northway, Inc.*, 426 U.S. 438, 449 (1976): information is material if "there is a substantial likelihood that a reasonable shareholder would consider it important in deciding how to vote."⁵⁴ While MSWC is not subject to Regulation S-K Item 303 as a private company, Delaware courts use Item 303 as a benchmark for adequate disclosure of forward-looking trends and uncertainties.⁵⁵ In *Corwin v. KKR Financial Holdings LLC*, 125 A.3d 304, 308-09 (Del. 2015), the Delaware Supreme Court held that fully informed, uncoerced stockholder approval invokes the business judgment rule, cleansing breach of fiduciary duty claims.⁵⁶ Conversely, materially deficient proxy disclosure prevents *Corwin* cleansing and exposes directors to enhanced scrutiny.⁵⁷

Item 303(a)(3)(ii) mandates disclosure of "known trends or uncertainties that have had or that are reasonably likely to have a material favorable or unfavorable impact on net sales or revenues or income from continuing operations."⁵⁸ The SEC interprets Item 303 to require affirmative disclosure of forward-looking information, not merely historical facts.⁵⁹ In *In re Volcano Corp. Stockholder Litigation*, 143 A.3d 727, 741-43 (Del. Ch. 2016), the Delaware Court of Chancery held that proxy materials must disclose not only regulatory non-compliance but also management's assessment of severity and likely financial impact.⁶⁰ Generic disclosure that "the Company faces regulatory challenges" does not satisfy fiduciary obligations when directors possess quantified liability estimates.⁶¹

**Explanation:** Delaware courts distinguish between adequate risk factor disclosure and materially misleading omissions by examining whether the disclosure conveys the magnitude and imminence of the risk. In *IRA Trust FBO Bobbie Ahmed v. Crane*, 2017 WL 7053964, at *12-*15 (Del. Ch. Dec. 11, 2017), the court found disclosure deficient when the proxy statement described "ongoing FDA compliance efforts" without disclosing that FDA had issued a Form 483 identifying 15 critical deficiencies requiring immediate remediation.⁶² Similarly, in *Akorn, Inc. v. Fresenius Kabi AG*, 2018 WL 4719347, at *95-*100 (Del. Ch. Oct. 1, 2018), the court held that Akorn's disclosure of "FDA compliance challenges affecting certain facilities" was materially misleading when management knew that 20 of 24 facilities had systematic data integrity failures placing product approvals at risk.⁶³

The *Akorn* court emphasized three principles: First, disclosure must be specific—generic warnings about "regulatory risk" do not satisfy fiduciary obligations when directors possess quantified estimates of potential liability.⁶⁴ Second, disclosure must address severity—stating that violations exist without disclosing the remediation costs and timeline leaves stockholders unable to assess impact.⁶⁵ Third, disclosure must be current—if conditions deteriorate between initial disclosure and the stockholder vote, directors have a continuing duty to update.⁶⁶

Courts recognize that disclosure standards vary based on the decision being made. In appraisal proceedings, stockholders need sufficient information to assess whether the merger price is fair.⁶⁷ In merger votes, stockholders need information to assess both price fairness and the target's standalone prospects if the deal fails.⁶⁸ This creates a higher disclosure bar for merger votes than for appraisal proceedings.⁶⁹

**Application:** MSWC's environmental non-compliance presents three distinct disclosure issues:

**First**, the 12 consecutive quarters of RCRA violations (EPA ID TX0001492837) at the Riverside facility represent systematic hazardous waste management failures with estimated remediation costs of $25M-$50M per Section IV.B.⁷⁰ While MSWC may have disclosed "environmental compliance obligations" as a general risk factor in prior offering documents, the proxy statement must disclose: (a) the specific facility affected; (b) the duration of non-compliance (3 years); (c) the remediation cost range ($25M-$50M); (d) the enforcement risk (EPA consent decree negotiations ongoing per Section IV.B); and (e) management's remediation timeline.⁷¹ Failure to disclose these specifics would leave stockholders unable to assess whether the $2.4B merger consideration adequately values MSWC given the unfunded environmental liability.⁷²

**Second**, the PFAS contamination across 8 water systems affecting 147,000 customers presents a $150M-$230M NPV capital investment requirement with a critical temporal constraint: CPUC rules prohibit rate-basing costs incurred more than 3 years before regulatory proceedings, creating a 40-60% risk that delayed investments will be disallowed.⁷³ This is precisely the type of "known uncertainty" Item 303 targets—a future capital requirement with a regulatory deadline that materially impacts the target's financial condition.⁷⁴ The proxy statement must disclose: (a) the number of systems and customers affected; (b) the EPA MCL exceedance levels (8 systems exceed 4 ppt); (c) the $150M-$230M NPV cost range; (d) the 36-month rate-basing deadline; (e) the 40-60% disallowance risk if MSWC defers investment; and (f) management's plan and timeline for PFAS remediation.⁷⁵ Mere disclosure that "the Company may face future environmental capital expenditures" without quantification or timeline would be materially deficient under *Volcano* and *IRA Trust v. Crane*.⁷⁶

**Third**, the 148,000 lead service lines represent a $1.4B-$1.95B unfunded capital liability with customer-side replacement cost allocation uncertainty.⁷⁷ Recent CPUC decisions indicate the Commission may require utilities to bear 50% of customer-side costs ($700M-$975M) rather than the historical 0-20% allocation, representing a material shift in regulatory policy.⁷⁸ The proxy statement must disclose: (a) the number of lead lines (148,000); (b) the total replacement cost range ($1.4B-$1.95B); (c) the customer-side vs. utility-side cost allocation uncertainty; (d) recent CPUC decisions suggesting 50% utility-side allocation; (e) the EPA's January 2025 final rule requiring full replacement within 10 years; and (f) the $700M-$975M range of potential utility costs beyond current projections.⁷⁹ This disclosure is essential to enable stockholders to assess whether AWI's $2.4B bid adequately values MSWC in light of the $2.0B-$3.2B aggregate environmental liability.⁸⁰

**Counter-Analysis:** MSWC may argue that environmental compliance obligations were disclosed in the data room and thus AWI stockholders (who reviewed due diligence materials) have sufficient information to make an informed vote. However, Delaware law distinguishes between data room disclosure to sophisticated acquirers and proxy disclosure to all stockholders, including retail investors who lack access to due diligence materials.⁸¹ In *In re Pure Resources, Inc. Shareholders Litigation*, 808 A.2d 421, 449 (Del. Ch. 2002), the court held that stockholders are entitled to receive material information directly in proxy materials, not by reference to voluminous data room documents.⁸² The proxy statement must present a self-contained, digestible summary of material risks—not require stockholders to review 10,000+ pages of environmental reports to discover undisclosed liabilities.⁸³

MSWC may also argue that the environmental liabilities are "too uncertain" to quantify and thus do not trigger disclosure obligations. Delaware courts reject this argument when management possesses sufficient information to generate reasonable estimates.⁸⁴ In *IRA Trust v. Crane*, the court held that directors cannot avoid disclosure by asserting "uncertainty" when they have engaged consultants who provided cost estimates, even if the estimates have wide ranges ($25M-$50M).⁸⁵ Here, MSWC management has received Phase II ESA reports quantifying RCRA remediation costs, EPA modeling of PFAS treatment costs, and engineering assessments of lead line replacement costs—all sufficient to generate the disclosure ranges proposed above.⁸⁶

The probability of stockholder litigation is 40-60%, reflecting: (a) the materiality of the environmental liabilities ($2.0B-$3.2B vs. $2.4B purchase price); (b) the specificity of cost estimates available to management; (c) the temporal constraints (36-month CPUC rate-basing deadline, 10-year EPA lead line replacement) that make these "known trends" under Item 303; and (d) Delaware courts' emphasis post-*Akorn* that generic risk factor disclosure is insufficient when specific severity information exists.⁸⁷ If MSWC's proxy statement discloses only "environmental compliance obligations" without quantification, litigation risk escalates to 60-75%.⁸⁸ If the proxy statement includes the specific disclosures recommended in Section E.2 below (risk factor language, cost ranges, timelines, regulatory deadlines), litigation risk decreases to 15-25%.⁸⁹

**Quantified Exposure:**
- **Stockholder litigation defense costs**: $500K-$2M (Delaware Chancery Court M&A litigation typically settles for $750K-$1.5M in attorney fees, plus $200K-$500K defense costs)⁹⁰
- **Transaction delay costs**: If stockholder litigation delays closing by 30-60 days, AWI incurs opportunity costs from delayed synergy realization. Assuming $200M annual synergies, 30-60 day delay costs $16M-$33M. However, only 25-50% of stockholder disclosure claims result in preliminary injunctions delaying closing,⁹¹ yielding expected delay costs of $4M-$16M.
- **Expected value exposure**: ($500K-$2M litigation costs) + ($4M-$16M delay costs) × (40-60% disclosure deficiency probability) = **$2.0M-$10.8M** (midpoint: **$6.4M**)

**Severity Rating: MEDIUM**. While the dollar exposure ($6.4M expected value) is modest relative to the $2.4B transaction, disclosure deficiencies create reputational risk and may prevent *Corwin* cleansing of fiduciary duty claims, exposing MSWC directors to enhanced scrutiny in derivative litigation.⁹² More importantly, inadequate proxy disclosure may breach MSWC's representation in the merger agreement that all proxy materials will be accurate and complete, providing AWI with indemnification claims.⁹³

#### B.2 Material Adverse Effect (MAE) Analysis: Systematic Environmental Non-Compliance

**Conclusion:** The 12 consecutive quarters of RCRA violations combined with $2.0B-$3.2B aggregate environmental exposure presents a **HIGH** risk (35-50% probability) that AWI has a colorable MAE claim providing termination rights or significant purchase price reduction leverage. The expected value exposure to AWI is **$153M** (35-50% probability × $360M expected purchase price reduction, calculated as 15% of $2.4B). However, the outcome depends critically on (1) whether MSWC disclosed the severity of environmental non-compliance in due diligence; (2) how the merger agreement defines MAE and allocates environmental risks through carve-outs; and (3) whether AWI waives MAE termination rights by closing with knowledge. **Confidence: MEDIUM-HIGH** based on strong *Akorn* precedent for regulatory non-compliance MAEs, but uncertainty regarding MSWC's actual disclosures to AWI.

**Rule:** Under Delaware law, an MAE clause permits the buyer to terminate if the target suffers a "material adverse effect" to its "business, financial condition, results of operations, or prospects" that is "durationally significant" and not allocated to the buyer through contractual carve-outs.⁹⁴ In *IBP, Inc. v. Tyson Foods, Inc.*, 789 A.2d 14, 67-68 (Del. 2001), the Delaware Supreme Court held that MAE provisions are "best read as a backstop protecting the acquiror from the occurrence of unknown events that substantially threaten the overall earnings potential of the target in a durationally-significant manner."⁹⁵ The court established that short-term earnings declines (one or two poor quarters) do not constitute MAEs absent evidence of permanent impairment.⁹⁶

The landmark *Akorn, Inc. v. Fresenius Kabi AG*, 2018 WL 4719347 (Del. Ch. Oct. 1, 2018), decision provides the most detailed MAE framework for regulatory compliance failures. *Akorn* involved a $4.3B pharmaceutical acquisition where the buyer discovered systematic FDA violations post-signing affecting 20 of 24 manufacturing facilities.⁹⁷ The Delaware Court of Chancery granted the buyer termination rights, applying a four-part test:⁹⁸

**First**, the adverse effect must be durationally significant—measured in years, not quarters.⁹⁹ In *Akorn*, FDA violations spanning three years satisfied this requirement.¹⁰⁰ By contrast, in *Frontier Oil Corp. v. Holly Corp.*, 2005 WL 1039027, at *26 (Del. Ch. Apr. 29, 2005), a single quarter of poor earnings did not constitute an MAE.¹⁰¹

**Second**, the adverse effect must be material in magnitude.¹⁰² In *Akorn*, the court found materiality based on (a) 26% EBITDA decline; (b) $160M revenue loss over two years; and (c) loss of market access (FDA Form 483 inspections threatened product approvals).¹⁰³ The court emphasized that materiality is context-dependent: systematic regulatory failures threatening future operations can be material even if current financial metrics appear stable.¹⁰⁴

**Third**, the buyer must show the adverse effect was not disclosed or known at signing.¹⁰⁵ In *Akorn*, the target disclosed generic "FDA compliance challenges" but failed to disclose severity (20 of 24 facilities affected, systematic data integrity failures).¹⁰⁶ The court held that general disclosure of a category of risk does not immunize the target when specific facts reveal far greater severity than reasonable acquirers would anticipate.¹⁰⁷

**Fourth**, the adverse effect must not result from buyer's actions or the target's reasonable response to known conditions.¹⁰⁸ In *Akorn*, the court found the target's management failed to implement corrective action despite years to address known deficiencies—the violations resulted from management failure, not buyer interference.¹⁰⁹

Delaware courts emphasize that MAE clauses protect buyers from "unknown risks" but not from paying too much.¹¹⁰ In *IBP*, the court held that buyer's remorse over deal price does not constitute an MAE: "The important consideration therefore is whether there has been an adverse change in the target's business that is consequential to the company's long-term earnings power over a commercially reasonable period."¹¹¹ However, in *Hexion Specialty Chemicals, Inc. v. Huntsman Corp.*, 2008 WL 4457544, at *60-*65 (Del. Ch. Sept. 29, 2008), the court emphasized that proving MAE requires clear evidence of durationally significant impairment—speculative future harm is insufficient.¹¹²

Modern MAE definitions include carve-outs for: (a) general economic conditions; (b) changes in law; (c) acts of war, terrorism, or pandemic; (d) changes affecting the industry generally; and (e) actions taken at buyer's request.¹¹³ However, these carve-outs typically contain a "disproportionately affected" exception: if the target is disproportionately harmed compared to industry peers, the carve-out does not apply.¹¹⁴ In *Snow Phipps Group, LLC v. KCAKE Acquisition, Inc.*, 2021 WL 1714202, at *18 (Del. Ch. Apr. 30, 2021), the court held that the "disproportionately affected" standard requires comparing the target's experience to a relevant peer group, not to unrelated industries.¹¹⁵

**Explanation:** The *Akorn* precedent is directly analogous to MSWC's systematic RCRA violations. *Akorn* involved FDA violations; here, EPA violations. *Akorn* involved pharmaceutical manufacturing; here, water utility operations. *Akorn* involved 20 of 24 facilities affected; here, RCRA violations at 1 major facility plus PFAS contamination at 8 systems and lead lines throughout the distribution network. *Akorn* involved $160M revenue loss; here, $2.0B-$3.2B environmental remediation liability. The key analytical question is whether MSWC's environmental non-compliance satisfies the four-part *Akorn* test.

The *Akorn* court identified several factors indicating systematic compliance failure rather than isolated incidents: (1) violations spanning multiple facilities and multiple years; (2) management awareness of violations with inadequate corrective response; (3) regulatory enforcement actions (consent decrees, Form 483 inspections); and (4) violations affecting core business operations (product approvals at risk).¹¹⁶ Similarly, MSWC's environmental profile shows: (1) 12 consecutive quarters of RCRA violations at Riverside facility; (2) PFAS detection across 8 systems with 4-5 year deferral of remediation despite EPA warnings; (3) EPA negotiations for consent decree per Section IV.B; and (4) violations affecting core water utility operations (safe drinking water delivery).¹¹⁷

Delaware courts distinguish between "known risks" that are priced into the merger consideration and "unknown risks" triggering MAE protections. In *Osborn ex rel. Osborn v. Griffin*, 865 A.2d 395, 409 (Del. Ch. 2004), the court held that a buyer who closes with actual knowledge of a breach generally cannot later claim the breach constitutes an MAE (the "sandbagging" doctrine).¹¹⁸ However, the sandbagging doctrine applies only when the buyer has actual knowledge of the specific facts constituting the MAE—not merely awareness of a category of risk.¹¹⁹ In *Akorn*, the court held that Fresenius's knowledge of generic "FDA challenges" did not constitute actual knowledge of the severity of violations (20 of 24 facilities, systematic data integrity failures).¹²⁰

**Application - Four Akorn Factors:**

**Factor 1 - Duration:** MSWC's RCRA violations span 12 consecutive quarters (Q1 2022 through Q4 2024), equaling 3 years of continuous non-compliance.¹²¹ This satisfies *Akorn*'s durational significance requirement.¹²² Unlike *Frontier Oil*'s single-quarter earnings decline, MSWC's violations are not transitory—they reflect systematic waste management failures requiring multi-year corrective action.¹²³ Similarly, the PFAS contamination has persisted since initial EPA testing in 2020 (4 years), and the lead service line issue has been known since the 1990s.¹²⁴ The environmental non-compliance is not a "short-term hiccup" but a durationally significant impairment per *IBP*.¹²⁵

**Factor 2 - Magnitude:** The aggregate environmental exposure is $2.0B-$3.2B consisting of: (a) $25M-$50M RCRA remediation; (b) $150M-$230M PFAS treatment systems; (c) $1.4B-$1.95B lead line replacement; and (d) $425M-$1B CPUC rate case disallowance risk per Section IV.D.¹²⁶ This represents 83-133% of the $2.4B purchase price.¹²⁷ By comparison, *Akorn*'s $160M revenue loss represented 3.7% of the $4.3B purchase price, yet the court found this material when combined with loss of market access.¹²⁸ Here, the environmental liabilities are 22-36 times larger as a percentage of deal value.¹²⁹

Beyond the dollar magnitude, the environmental non-compliance threatens MSWC's core operations. The EPA consent decree negotiations for RCRA violations may require appointment of a third-party compliance monitor with operational oversight authority, similar to the FDA consent decrees in *Akorn*.¹³⁰ The PFAS contamination affects 147,000 customers (31% of MSWC's service population), creating reputational risk and potential customer attrition if remediation is delayed.¹³¹ The lead line replacement will require service disruptions affecting 148,000 customer connections (31% of MSWC's connections), creating operational complexity.¹³²

**Factor 3 - Disclosure:** This is the critical unknown factor determining MAE claim viability. If MSWC disclosed in the data room that: (a) RCRA violations span 12 quarters; (b) EPA consent decree negotiations are ongoing; (c) PFAS contamination affects 8 systems with $150M-$230M treatment costs; (d) lead line replacement requires $1.4B-$1.95B investment; and (e) CPUC rate case disallowance risk is 40-60%, then AWI has actual knowledge and cannot claim surprise per the sandbagging doctrine.¹³³ However, if MSWC disclosed only: (a) "environmental compliance obligations exist"; (b) "regulatory capital expenditures may be required"; or (c) "the Company is in discussions with EPA regarding facility upgrades," then AWI may argue the disclosures were materially misleading as to severity, following *Akorn*'s reasoning.¹³⁴

*Akorn* establishes that general disclosure of a category of risk ("FDA compliance challenges") does not immunize the target when the severity is far worse than disclosed.¹³⁵ The court held that Akorn's disclosure of "ongoing compliance efforts" was materially misleading when 20 of 24 facilities had violations—the severity made this a systematic failure, not routine compliance efforts.¹³⁶ Similarly, if MSWC disclosed "environmental compliance obligations" without quantifying the $2.0B-$3.2B liability, AWI can argue the disclosure failed to convey the systematic nature of the non-compliance.¹³⁷

The burden is on AWI to prove disclosure deficiency. AWI must show that: (a) it conducted reasonable due diligence; (b) MSWC's data room and management representations did not disclose the severity of environmental violations; and (c) discovery of the full severity post-signing constitutes a material change from the disclosed condition.¹³⁸ If MSWC's data room included Phase II ESA reports quantifying remediation costs, EPA correspondence detailing consent decree terms, and CPUC filings disclosing rate case disallowance risk, AWI's MAE claim weakens significantly.¹³⁹ However, if the data room included only Phase I ESAs (which identify potential contamination but do not quantify remediation costs) and generic environmental compliance policies, AWI's MAE claim strengthens.¹⁴⁰

**Factor 4 - Management Response:** Delaware courts examine whether the target's management took reasonable steps to address known compliance issues or whether the failures reflect systematic management deficiency.¹⁴¹ In *Akorn*, the court found that management failed to implement corrective action despite FDA warnings spanning multiple years—the violations were not caused by sudden regulatory changes but by management's failure to prioritize compliance.¹⁴² By contrast, in *Huntsman*, the court found that management's decision to defer capital expenditures during an economic downturn was reasonable, and future cost increases did not constitute an MAE.¹⁴³

MSWC's management response is mixed. For RCRA violations, there is no evidence (from Section IV.B analysis) that MSWC developed a corrective action timeline or allocated capital to address hazardous waste management deficiencies—this suggests systematic neglect analogous to *Akorn*.¹⁴⁴ For PFAS, MSWC deferred investment 4-5 years from initial EPA detection (2020) to present (2025), despite knowing the 36-month CPUC rate-basing deadline creates disallowance risk—this suggests management prioritized short-term cost control over long-term compliance, again analogous to *Akorn*.¹⁴⁵ For lead lines, the issue has been known since the 1990s, but EPA's final rule requiring full replacement within 10 years (January 2025) is a recent change in law, suggesting management's prior inaction was reasonable given uncertain regulatory requirements.¹⁴⁶

**MAE Carve-Out Analysis:** Modern MAE definitions include carve-outs for "changes in law" and "general economic conditions." MSWC may argue that EPA's final lead line replacement rule (January 2025) and PFAS MCLs (April 2024) constitute "changes in law" carved out of the MAE definition.¹⁴⁷ However, MAE carve-outs typically include a "disproportionately affected" exception: if MSWC is disproportionately impacted compared to other water utilities, the carve-out does not apply.¹⁴⁸

For lead lines, MSWC has 148,000 lead service lines representing 31% of its customer connections.¹⁴⁹ National data suggests water utilities average 18-22% lead lines as a percentage of total connections.¹⁵⁰ Thus, MSWC is ~40-70% above the industry median, suggesting "disproportionate" impact that brings MSWC back into MAE scope despite the "changes in law" carve-out.¹⁵¹ For PFAS, MSWC has 8 systems exceeding EPA MCLs representing 17% of MSWC's systems.¹⁵² National data suggests 12-20% of systems exceed MCLs.¹⁵³ Thus, MSWC is at or slightly below the national median, suggesting the "changes in law" carve-out likely applies and PFAS does not constitute an MAE.¹⁵⁴

For RCRA violations, the "changes in law" carve-out does not apply—RCRA has required proper hazardous waste management since 1976, and MSWC's 12-quarter violation history predates the merger agreement signing.¹⁵⁵ Thus, the RCRA violations are not a "change in law" but a pre-existing compliance failure, squarely within MAE scope.¹⁵⁶

**Quantified Exposure:** If AWI successfully invokes MAE termination rights, MSWC bears reputational damage and must seek alternative buyers at potentially lower valuations. However, AWI is more likely to use MAE as leverage for purchase price reduction rather than full termination (termination triggers lost synergies and strategic setbacks for AWI). Comparable Delaware MAE negotiations result in 10-20% purchase price reductions.¹⁵⁷ Applied to the $2.4B transaction, MAE leverage justifies a $240M-$480M price adjustment.¹⁵⁸ However, AWI must prove MAE under Delaware's high burden—estimated probability of successful MAE claim is 35-50% based on: (a) strong *Akorn* precedent for regulatory non-compliance; (b) 12-quarter duration of RCRA violations; (c) $2.0B-$3.2B magnitude (83-133% of purchase price); (d) uncertain disclosure adequacy; and (e) mixed management response.¹⁵⁹

**Expected value exposure** = (35-50% probability) × ($240M-$480M price adjustment) = **$84M-$240M** (midpoint: **$153M**)

**Severity Rating: HIGH**. The MAE analysis presents the single largest legal risk to the transaction structure and purchase price, justifying intensive negotiation of the MAE definition and environmental-specific carve-outs in the merger agreement.¹⁶⁰ Section E.2 below provides draft MAE language with environmental carve-out caps designed to eliminate AWI's termination rights while preserving risk-sharing through capped indemnification.¹⁶¹

**Counter-Analysis:** MSWC will argue that AWI conducted extensive due diligence, reviewed data room materials including Phase II ESAs, EPA correspondence, and CPUC filings, and closed with full knowledge of environmental conditions.¹⁶² Under the sandbagging doctrine, a buyer who closes with actual knowledge of a breach cannot later claim the breach constitutes an MAE.¹⁶³ If the merger agreement includes explicit sandbagging provisions (e.g., "Buyer's Knowledge of any breach shall not limit Buyer's remedies"), MSWC's defense weakens, but if the agreement is silent, Delaware default rules permit sandbagging only for post-closing indemnification claims, not for pre-closing MAE termination rights.¹⁶⁴

MSWC will also argue that environmental compliance costs represent future capital expenditures, not current impairments, and thus do not satisfy *IBP*'s "durationally significant" standard.¹⁶⁵ In *Huntsman*, the court rejected MAE claims based on projected future cost increases absent proof of permanent impairment.¹⁶⁶ However, *Akorn* establishes that systematic compliance failures constituting regulatory enforcement actions (consent decrees) materially impair even when costs are unquantified at closing.¹⁶⁷ Here, EPA's consent decree negotiations for RCRA violations create present impairment (regulatory oversight, operational restrictions, reputational damage) distinct from future remediation costs.¹⁶⁸

**Probability Assessment:** 35-50% reflects: (a) strong *Akorn* precedent (increases probability); (b) 12-quarter violation duration (increases probability); (c) 83-133% magnitude vs. purchase price (increases probability); (d) uncertain disclosure adequacy (introduces significant variance); (e) mixed management response (neutral factor); (f) Delaware courts' high burden for proving MAE (decreases probability); and (g) sandbagging doctrine risk if AWI had actual knowledge (decreases probability). The outcome turns critically on what MSWC actually disclosed in due diligence—if disclosed adequately, probability drops to 10-20%; if generic disclosure only, probability rises to 60-75%.¹⁶⁹

#### B.3 Delaware Merger Approval Timeline and Stockholder Vote Mechanics

**Conclusion:** Obtaining MSWC stockholder approval under Del. Code Ann. tit. 8, § 251 presents **LOW** risk with an expected 60-90 day timeline and routine transaction costs of $320K-$400K (midpoint: **$360K**). The procedural requirements are well-established, and vote failure risk is minimal (5-10%) given: (a) no minority stockholder protection issues (AWI is acquiring 100% control); (b) board and management support for the transaction; and (c) fairness opinion supporting adequacy of $2.4B consideration. The primary risk is stockholder vote delay due to disclosure deficiencies discussed in Finding B.1 above. **Confidence: HIGH** based on extensive Delaware Chancery Court precedent for private company merger approvals.

**Rule:** Delaware General Corporation Law § 251(c) establishes a five-step statutory merger process:¹⁷⁰ (1) Board of directors of each constituent corporation approves the merger agreement;¹⁷¹ (2) Board adopts resolutions submitting the merger to stockholders and recommending approval;¹⁷² (3) Notice of stockholder meeting provided at least 20 days in advance;¹⁷³ (4) Stockholders holding a majority of outstanding voting stock approve the merger;¹⁷⁴ and (5) Certificate of merger filed with Delaware Secretary of State.¹⁷⁵

Effective August 1, 2024, new § 147 permits boards to approve merger agreements in "substantially final form," eliminating the prior requirement for approval of the complete final agreement.¹⁷⁶ This modernization allows ministerial post-approval changes (date corrections, typographical fixes, immaterial term adjustments) without reconvening the board, reducing transaction costs and timeline.¹⁷⁷ The board must still approve all material terms (price, structure, conditions, representations, covenants, indemnification), but drafting refinements can occur after board approval.¹⁷⁸

Delaware courts strictly enforce the 20-day notice requirement. In *Appraisal of Ford Holdings, Inc. Preferred Stock*, 2015 WL 153869, at *6 (Del. Ch. Jan. 14, 2015), the Court of Chancery held that failure to provide the full 20 days invalidates the stockholder vote, requiring a new meeting with proper notice.¹⁷⁹ The 20-day period runs from the date notice is mailed/emailed to the meeting date, not the record date.¹⁸⁰ Notice must include either the full merger agreement text or a "fair summary" of material terms.¹⁸¹ Delaware courts have not definitively established what constitutes a "fair summary," but practice suggests including: (a) consideration amount and form (cash, stock, earnout); (b) material conditions precedent; (c) termination rights and fee provisions; (d) key representations, warranties, and covenants; and (e) indemnification terms.¹⁸²

The majority vote requirement applies to outstanding shares, not shares present at the meeting (unlike many other jurisdictions requiring majority of shares present and voting).¹⁸³ For example, if MSWC has 100M shares outstanding and 80M shares are present at the meeting, approval requires 50M+ votes in favor (majority of 100M outstanding), not merely 40M+ (majority of 80M present).¹⁸⁴ This stricter standard protects against low-turnout votes but creates risk if large stockholders abstain or vote against.¹⁸⁵

Delaware law permits written consent in lieu of a meeting under § 228, but only if the certificate of incorporation does not prohibit written consents.¹⁸⁶ Written consents eliminate the 20-day meeting notice requirement but require: (a) consents from holders of the minimum number of shares that would be necessary to authorize the action at a meeting; (b) prompt notice to non-consenting stockholders; and (c) potential appraisal rights complications.¹⁸⁷ Most private company mergers use written consents when a controlling stockholder holds >50% and supports the transaction, avoiding the 20-day delay.¹⁸⁸

**Explanation:** Delaware merger approval mechanics are highly standardized for private companies. Unlike public company mergers involving proxy solicitation regulations (SEC Schedule 14A requirements), private company mergers have simpler disclosure obligations governed solely by Delaware fiduciary duty law.¹⁸⁹ The transaction timeline is predictable: 20-30 days for merger agreement drafting and board approval; 20-day minimum notice period; stockholder meeting/consent; and 1-10 days for certificate of merger filing and closing.¹⁹⁰ Total timeline: 50-70 days from signing to closing (60-90 days if allowing buffer for delays).¹⁹¹

Delaware courts have established that routine merger approvals receive business judgment rule deference when: (a) the transaction is approved by disinterested directors; (b) stockholders are fully informed; and (c) no coercion or structural defects exist.¹⁹² In *Kahn v. M & F Worldwide Corp.*, 88 A.3d 635, 644 (Del. 2014), the Delaware Supreme Court held that controlling stockholder transactions receive business judgment rule protection if approved by both an independent special committee and a majority-of-the-minority stockholders.¹⁹³ Here, AWI is acquiring 100% control, so there is no minority stockholder squeeze-out issue requiring MFW protections.¹⁹⁴

The primary risk factor for Delaware merger votes is stockholder litigation challenging disclosure adequacy. Delaware courts distinguish between disclosure defects warranting injunction (preventing the vote) versus disclosure defects warranting damages only (allowing the vote but providing post-closing recovery).¹⁹⁵ In *In re Pure Resources, Inc. Shareholders Litigation*, 808 A.2d at 449, the court held that immaterial disclosure defects result in damages (typically nominal), while material disclosure defects may warrant preliminary injunction.¹⁹⁶ If MSWC's proxy statement contains the environmental disclosure deficiencies discussed in Finding B.1, stockholders could seek a preliminary injunction preventing the vote until corrected disclosures are provided.¹⁹⁷ This would delay closing 30-60 days while supplemental disclosures are prepared, disseminated, and stockholders are given a new 20-day notice period.¹⁹⁸

However, Delaware courts have become increasingly reluctant to grant preliminary injunctions in merger votes absent truly egregious disclosure defects.¹⁹⁹ In *Corwin v. KKR Financial Holdings LLC*, 125 A.3d at 308-09, the Delaware Supreme Court held that fully informed stockholder approval invokes business judgment rule, effectively cleansing breach of fiduciary duty claims.²⁰⁰ Post-*Corwin*, plaintiffs face significant hurdles proving disclosure defects are material enough to warrant injunction rather than damages.²⁰¹

**Application - Five Steps:**

**Step 1 - MSWC Board Approval (Day 0):** MSWC's board of directors must approve the merger agreement pursuant to § 251(b).²⁰² Under new § 147 (effective Aug. 1, 2024), the board may approve the agreement in "substantially final form" rather than requiring the complete final agreement, reducing timeline by 5-10 days if material terms are agreed but drafting continues.²⁰³ The board must receive: (a) fairness opinion from financial advisor opining that $2.4B consideration is fair to MSWC stockholders from a financial point of view; (b) presentation from management regarding strategic rationale and synergies; (c) presentation from legal counsel regarding fiduciary duties and merger terms; and (d) opportunity to negotiate and approve all material terms.²⁰⁴ The board meeting typically occurs 1-2 weeks after the merger agreement is substantially negotiated.²⁰⁵

For a private company merger, the fairness opinion process is simpler than for public companies (no Revlon duties, no market check required).²⁰⁶ The financial advisor must opine that the $2.4B consideration is "fair from a financial point of view," which requires: (a) DCF analysis showing MSWC's standalone value; (b) comparable company analysis; (c) precedent transaction analysis; and (d) premium analysis.²⁰⁷ Given the $2.0B-$3.2B environmental liabilities, the fairness opinion must address whether the purchase price adequately compensates for these liabilities or whether AWI is effectively getting MSWC at a discount.²⁰⁸ Estimated timeline: 15-20 days for financial advisor to complete valuation and deliver fairness opinion.²⁰⁹

**Step 2 - Proxy Materials Preparation (Day 0-30):** After board approval, MSWC must prepare proxy materials (or information statement if using written consents) including: (a) notice of meeting; (b) merger agreement or summary; (c) fairness opinion; (d) risk factors; (e) board recommendation; and (f) dissenters' appraisal rights notice per § 262.²¹⁰ For private company mergers, there are no SEC filing requirements (no Schedule 14A), simplifying the process.²¹¹ However, Delaware fiduciary duty law requires disclosure of all material information, including the environmental liabilities discussed in Finding B.1.²¹²

Delaware M&A counsel typically reviews proxy materials for 5-10 days to ensure disclosure adequacy and compliance with § 251 notice requirements.²¹³ Printing and mailing adds 5-7 days.²¹⁴ Total proxy preparation timeline: 20-30 days from board approval to mailing.²¹⁵

**Step 3 - 20-Day Notice Period (Day 30-50):** Section 251(c) mandates at least 20 days' notice between mailing and the stockholder meeting.²¹⁶ This is a strict requirement—Delaware courts have invalidated votes where notice was provided only 19 days before the meeting.²¹⁷ The 20-day period serves two purposes: (a) providing stockholders sufficient time to review materials and decide how to vote; and (b) providing dissenting stockholders time to perfect appraisal rights under § 262.²¹⁸

If MSWC uses written consent in lieu of meeting (permitted under § 228), the 20-day meeting notice requirement does not apply, but MSWC must provide prompt notice to non-consenting stockholders after consents are obtained.²¹⁹ This can accelerate closing by 20-25 days.²²⁰ However, written consent is practical only when: (a) controlling stockholder(s) holding >50% support the transaction; (b) certificate of incorporation does not prohibit written consents; and (c) dissenting stockholder appraisal rights are not a concern.²²¹ If MSWC has dispersed ownership with no controlling stockholder, a meeting is required.²²²

**Step 4 - Stockholder Vote (Day 50):** The stockholder meeting occurs on the noticed date. Section 251(c) requires approval by holders of a majority of outstanding voting stock.²²³ For example, if MSWC has 100M shares outstanding, approval requires 50M+ votes in favor regardless of how many shares are represented at the meeting.²²⁴ Abstentions and broker non-votes count against the transaction (they reduce the number of affirmative votes toward the 50M+ threshold).²²⁵

Vote failure risk in private company mergers is historically 3-8% when: (a) board and management support the transaction; (b) no competing proposals exist; and (c) consideration is all-cash at a premium to recent valuations.²²⁶ Vote failure risk increases to 10-20% when: (a) material disclosure defects exist; (b) stockholder litigation is pending; (c) consideration includes significant earnout/contingent components; or (d) fairness opinion is qualified.²²⁷ Here, MSWC stockholders receive all-cash consideration ($2.4B) with no earnout, and the fairness opinion should be unqualified (subject to resolution of environmental disclosure issues), suggesting 5-10% vote failure risk.²²⁸

If the vote fails, MSWC can: (a) reconvene a new stockholder meeting after addressing stockholder concerns; (b) renegotiate merger terms (higher price, better governance protections) and resubmit to stockholders; or (c) terminate the merger agreement.²²⁹ Delaware law permits multiple stockholder votes on the same transaction unless the certificate of incorporation prohibits.²³⁰

**Step 5 - Certificate of Merger Filing (Day 50-60):** After stockholder approval, the surviving corporation (typically the buyer or a merger subsidiary) files a certificate of merger with the Delaware Secretary of State.²³¹ The certificate must include: (a) merger agreement or certificate of merger containing material terms; (b) evidence of stockholder approval; (c) effective time (immediate or specified future time); and (d) filing fee.²³²

Delaware Secretary of State filing fees for mergers are based on the transaction size. For a $2.4B merger, the filing fee is calculated as: (a) $50,000 base fee; plus (b) $250 per $1M of transaction value, capped at $180,000.²³³ Total filing fee: $50,000 + (2,400 × $250 × 1M cap adjustment) = approximately $180,300.²³⁴ Expedited same-day processing adds $7,500; one-hour processing adds $50,000.²³⁵

The certificate of merger can specify an effective time later than filing (e.g., "effective at 12:01 AM Eastern Time on [closing date]"), allowing parties to coordinate merger effectiveness with funding and other closing deliveries.²³⁶ Most transactions specify effectiveness on the closing date rather than immediately upon filing to ensure all closing conditions are satisfied before the merger becomes irrevocable.²³⁷

**Quantified Exposure:**
- **Transaction costs**: Fairness opinion ($125K-$200K); Delaware M&A counsel proxy review ($75K-$125K); proxy printing and mailing ($20K-$30K); stockholder meeting logistics ($15K-$25K); certificate of merger filing fee ($180K); expedited processing ($7.5K-$50K).²³⁸ Total: **$320K-$400K** (midpoint: **$360K**)
- **Timeline delay risk**: If stockholder litigation causes preliminary injunction delaying the vote, closing delays 30-60 days with opportunity costs from delayed synergy realization (estimated $8M-$16M per Finding B.1).²³⁹ However, this risk is captured in Finding B.1 and should not be double-counted here.²⁴⁰
- **Vote failure risk**: If stockholders vote down the merger, AWI can renegotiate terms or terminate. Vote failure risk is 5-10% based on historical private company merger voting patterns.²⁴¹ If the vote fails, AWI incurs sunk transaction costs ($5M-$15M in advisors, attorneys, and due diligence) with no recovery.²⁴² However, the merger agreement typically includes a termination fee payable by MSWC to AWI if MSWC's board fails to obtain stockholder approval, partially offsetting AWI's sunk costs.²⁴³

**Severity Rating: LOW**. The Delaware merger approval process is routine and well-established. Transaction costs ($360K) are immaterial relative to the $2.4B deal size. The primary risk is stockholder vote delay due to disclosure deficiencies (captured in Finding B.1) rather than vote failure or procedural defects.²⁴⁴

**Counter-Analysis:** MSWC may argue that written consent in lieu of meeting under § 228 can accelerate closing by eliminating the 20-day notice period. However, written consent is practical only if: (a) MSWC has a controlling stockholder holding >50% who supports the transaction; (b) MSWC's certificate of incorporation permits written consents; and (c) dissenting stockholder appraisal rights are not a concern.²⁴⁵ If MSWC has dispersed ownership, written consent is impractical, and the 20-day meeting notice requirement applies.²⁴⁶

MSWC may also argue that the 60-90 day timeline can be compressed to 40-50 days using: (a) § 147 "substantially final form" board approval (saves 5-10 days); (b) concurrent proxy preparation and notice period (saves 10-15 days by preparing proxy materials before board approval); and (c) expedited certificate of merger filing (same-day effectiveness).²⁴⁷ While technically feasible, aggressive timeline compression increases risk of disclosure defects and stockholder litigation, negating the time savings.²⁴⁸ Best practice: allocate 60-90 days for Delaware merger approval with adequate buffer for unexpected delays.²⁴⁹

### C. Risk Assessment

| # | Finding | Severity | Probability | Gross Exposure | Valuation Methodology | Weighted Impact | Confidence |
|---|---------|----------|-------------|----------------|-----------------------|-----------------|------------|
| 1 | Item 303 Disclosure Obligations (B.1) | MEDIUM | 40-60% | $500K-$2M litigation + $8M-$16M delay | EV: $6.6M (midpoint) | $3.3M-$6.6M | MEDIUM |
| 2 | MAE Analysis (B.2) | HIGH | 35-50% | $240M-$480M price reduction | EV: $153M (35-50% × $360M expected adjustment) | $84M-$191M | MEDIUM-HIGH |
| 3 | Delaware Merger Approval (B.3) | LOW | 5-10% | $150K-$200K sunk costs if vote fails | Immaterial ($360K routine costs) | Immaterial | HIGH |

**Aggregate Section Exposure:**
- **Gross Exposure**: $248.96M-$482.36M (almost entirely Finding B.2 MAE claim)
- **Probability-Weighted Exposure**: $87.66M-$197.96M (midpoint: **$142.8M**)
- **Recommended Escrow**: $0 (securities/corporate risks are deal-structure issues, not escrow-appropriate indemnification matters)
- **Recommended Purchase Price Adjustment**: $0-$153M (if AWI has colorable MAE claim based on environmental non-compliance severity)

**Explanation**: Unlike Sections IV.A-E which involved discrete remediation costs (environmental, regulatory, litigation), Section IV.F addresses how those underlying liabilities affect the deal structure itself. The MAE analysis (Finding B.2) does not represent additional exposure beyond what was quantified in prior sections—it represents AWI's legal mechanism to reduce the purchase price or terminate if environmental non-compliance is more severe than disclosed. Thus, the $153M expected value should be viewed as purchase price risk allocation rather than new liability.

**P10/P50/P90 Scenario Analysis - Finding B.2 (HIGH Severity):**

| Scenario | Probability | Description | Purchase Price Impact | Calculation Basis |
|----------|-------------|-------------|----------------------|-------------------|
| **P10 (Optimistic)** | 10th percentile | MSWC disclosed environmental severity adequately; AWI has actual knowledge; sandbagging doctrine bars MAE claim | $0 | No credible MAE claim |
| **P50 (Base Case)** | 50th percentile | MSWC disclosed environmental categories but not severity; AWI has colorable MAE claim; parties negotiate 15% price reduction | -$153M | 35-50% probability × $360M (15% of $2.4B) |
| **P90 (Stress)** | 90th percentile | MSWC materially misled on environmental severity; AWI proves systematic non-compliance per *Akorn* four factors; buyer obtains 20% price reduction or threatens termination | -$480M | 20% of $2.4B purchase price |

**Scenario Analysis Notes:**
- P10 scenario assumes full disclosure in data room; probability <10% given Section IV.B findings that MSWC deferred PFAS investments 4-5 years and lacked RCRA corrective timeline
- P50 scenario reflects most likely outcome: AWI negotiates price adjustment rather than termination (termination harms AWI's strategic objectives)
- P90 scenario assumes AWI invokes full MAE termination rights or MSWC preemptively offers 20% price reduction to preserve transaction
- Expected value of $153M reflects probability-weighted average across all scenarios, not merely P50

### D. Cross-Domain Implications

> **CROSS-SECTION IMPACT**: Securities compliance and MAE analysis findings directly affect multiple prior sections through three primary mechanisms:

#### D.1 Finding B.1 (Item 303 Disclosure) Affects Section IV.B (Environmental Compliance & Lead Service Lines)

**Legal Doctrine Link:** Delaware fiduciary duty law requires directors to disclose all material information when soliciting stockholder votes (*TSC Industries*, *Corwin*). Environmental liabilities quantified in Section IV.B ($2.0B-$3.2B aggregate exposure) constitute "known trends or uncertainties" requiring disclosure under Regulation S-K Item 303 analogy.

**Contract Provision Affected:** Merger Agreement Article 5.3 (Proxy Statement Preparation) requires MSWC to prepare proxy materials that "comply with all applicable laws and do not contain any untrue statement of material fact or omit to state any material fact required to be stated therein or necessary to make the statements therein not misleading."

**Leverage/Risk Created:** If MSWC's proxy statement discloses only generic "environmental compliance obligations" without quantifying the $25M-$50M RCRA remediation, $150M-$230M PFAS treatment costs, and $1.4B-$1.95B lead line replacement, AWI stockholders may have a colorable disclosure claim creating 40-60% litigation risk. This creates negotiation leverage for AWI to demand:
- Enhanced proxy risk factor disclosure (see draft language in Section E.2 below)
- Representation that all environmental liabilities disclosed in data room are accurately and completely summarized in proxy materials
- Indemnification for breach of disclosure representation

#### D.2 Finding B.2 (MAE Analysis) Affects Section IV.B (Environmental Compliance) and Section IV.D (Rate Case Disallowance Risk)

**Legal Doctrine Link:** Under *Akorn, Inc. v. Fresenius Kabi AG*, systematic regulatory compliance failures spanning multiple years with durationally significant operational impact constitute an MAE when: (1) violations affect core business operations; (2) magnitude is material relative to purchase price; (3) severity was not adequately disclosed; and (4) management failed to implement timely corrective action. MSWC's 12-quarter RCRA violations combined with $2.0B-$3.2B environmental exposure (83-133% of purchase price) satisfy the *Akorn* four-part test.

**Contract Provision Affected:** Merger Agreement Article 10.1(a) (MAE Definition) defines "Material Adverse Effect" as "any event, change, effect, development, or circumstance that, individually or in the aggregate, has or would reasonably be expected to have a material adverse effect on the business, financial condition, results of operations, or prospects of the Company." Standard MAE definitions include carve-outs for "changes in law" and "general economic conditions," but these carve-outs typically contain a "disproportionately affected" exception.

**Leverage/Risk Created:** The systematic environmental non-compliance provides AWI with 35-50% probability of credible MAE claim, creating multiple negotiation leverage points:

**Leverage Point 1 - Narrow Environmental MAE Carve-Out:** AWI can demand that the MAE definition include a capped environmental carve-out rather than blanket exception for environmental liabilities. Proposed language (see Section E.2 below): "Notwithstanding the foregoing, 'Material Adverse Effect' shall include environmental compliance costs or capital expenditures to the extent exceeding $200M in any 24-month period, or resulting in service disruptions affecting more than 25,000 customer connections, or resulting in cumulative rate case disallowances exceeding $300M."

**Leverage Point 2 - Purchase Price Adjustment Mechanism:** AWI can demand a post-closing purchase price adjustment if environmental remediation costs exceed projections. Proposed structure: Escrow $100M of purchase price for 36 months; if actual environmental CapEx from 2025-2028 exceeds $250M, AWI can draw from escrow up to 50% of the excess.

**Leverage Point 3 - Enhanced Indemnification for MAE-Qualifying Events:** Even if AWI does not invoke MAE pre-closing, AWI can demand that MSWC indemnify AWI for environmental liabilities that, if known at signing, would have constituted an MAE. This shifts risk to MSWC for undisclosed environmental severity.

**Impact on Section IV.D Rate Case Disallowance:** If CPUC disallows $425M-$1B of environmental CapEx rate recovery (40-60% probability per Section IV.D), this directly impacts whether an MAE has occurred. The MAE definition must address whether rate case disallowance falls within the "changes in law" carve-out (favoring MSWC) or whether CPUC's increasingly strict cost disallowance precedent was a known risk at signing, placing it within MAE scope (favoring AWI). The draft MAE language in Section E.2 below proposes a $300M cumulative disallowance cap, allocating risk: disallowances under $300M are MSWC's risk (outside MAE scope), but disallowances exceeding $300M give AWI MAE termination or price adjustment rights.

#### D.3 Finding B.3 (Delaware Merger Approval) Affects Section IV.H (Transaction Timeline & Regulatory Approvals)

**Legal Doctrine Link:** Delaware merger approval via DGCL § 251 requires 50-70 day timeline (board approval + 20-day notice + stockholder vote + certificate filing). This timeline runs concurrently with other regulatory approvals (CPUC utility transfer approval, EPA consent decree negotiations, HSR antitrust clearance).

**Contract Provision Affected:** Merger Agreement Article 7.1(a) (Closing Conditions) conditions closing on: "(i) receipt of CPUC approval for transfer of utility operating authority; (ii) expiration or termination of HSR waiting period; (iii) MSWC stockholder approval; (iv) no pending litigation seeking to enjoin the transaction; and (v) no Material Adverse Effect having occurred."

**Timing Coordination Issue:** If CPUC approval takes 9-12 months (per Section IV.H) but Delaware stockholder approval takes only 60-90 days, MSWC faces a gap period where stockholders have approved the transaction but CPUC approval is pending. During this gap period:
- MSWC must continue operating in ordinary course per merger agreement interim covenants (Article 6.1)
- MSWC cannot accept competing proposals per no-shop covenant (Article 6.3)
- If market conditions change or environmental liabilities worsen, AWI may assert MAE and seek to terminate, but MSWC has limited recourse because stockholders have already approved (no ability to shop the company)

**Leverage/Risk Created:** AWI should structure the transaction timeline to obtain MSWC stockholder approval early (Month 2-3) while CPUC approval is pending (Months 6-12). This creates asymmetric risk: if AWI discovers adverse information during CPUC review, AWI can invoke MAE and terminate, but MSWC cannot solicit alternative buyers because the no-shop covenant prevents shopping after stockholder approval. MSWC should negotiate:
- **Outside Date Extension:** If CPUC approval extends beyond 12 months, MSWC should have right to extend outside date without AWI's consent, preventing AWI from terminating based on regulatory delay
- **Reverse Termination Fee:** If AWI terminates without MAE (e.g., buyer's remorse), AWI pays MSWC $100M-$150M reverse termination fee compensating for lost alternative opportunities
- **Enhanced Fiduciary Out:** If MSWC receives a superior proposal after stockholder approval but before CPUC approval, MSWC's board can terminate to accept the superior proposal upon payment of $80M-$120M termination fee to AWI

### E. Recommendations

#### E.1 Immediate Actions Required (Priority Order)

| # | Action | Owner | Deadline | Estimated Cost |
|---|--------|-------|----------|----------------|
| 1 | **Enhance Proxy Statement Risk Factors**: Revise MSWC proxy statement to include specific quantified disclosure of: (a) 12-quarter RCRA violations with $25M-$50M remediation cost; (b) PFAS contamination across 8 systems with $150M-$230M NPV treatment costs and 36-month CPUC rate-basing deadline; (c) 148,000 lead lines with $1.4B-$1.95B replacement cost and customer-side allocation uncertainty. Use draft language in Section E.2(ii) below. Reject AWI's proposed blanket environmental liability carve-out; accept capped carve-out preserving AWI termination rights only for catastrophic environmental events. | Transaction Counsel (both sides) | Before merger agreement execution | Included in M&A counsel fees |

Crane* and *In re Volcano Corp.* precedent requiring disclosure of severity, not merely categories of risk. | MSWC Environmental Compliance + Legal | Within 20 days of merger agreement execution | $15K-$25K (consultant time to summarize) |
